Aliases raise several issues pertaining to naming, deletion and accounting.
¬ä
Naming: A Ô¨Åle with several aliases no longer seems to have a ‚Äò‚Äòtrue‚Äô‚Äô name. Is the
mail Ô¨Åle really /smith.courses/mail or /smith.thesis/mail? We
can increase the confusion by giving the same Ô¨Åle different local names in the two
directories, such as mail and letters. We could even put the two aliases in
the same directory, as long as the local names are different.
If we are willing to distinguish Ô¨Åles from names, as our Ô¨Ågures show, we
can Ô¨Ånd this situation useful. A Ô¨Åle may have multiple names. For example, every
student in a class might be expected to build a Ô¨Åle called project.mod. The
teacher may wish to build an alias for each of these Ô¨Åles in the teacher‚Äôs directory.
The local name for each Ô¨Åle in the teacher‚Äôs directory could refer to the student:
brown.mod, asaithambi.mod, and mogolescu.mod.
¬ã
Deletion: If the Ô¨Åle is deleted under one of its aliases, should it disappear under all
aliases? If so, then it is necessary to Ô¨Ånd all the directories that contain aliases for
the deleted Ô¨Åle and remove those aliases as well. To make this search efÔ¨Åcient, all
190
File Structures
Chapter 6

the aliases of a Ô¨Åle could be listed in a common place, perhaps along with the con-
tents of the Ô¨Åle itself.
A nice alternative is to allow the Ô¨Åle to remain intact until the last alias is
deleted. A reference count can be stored with the Ô¨Åle to indicate how many aliases
it has. Most Ô¨Åles have just one alias, so they have reference count 1. Files that
have been placed in several directories (or several times in the same directory)
have a higher reference count. Making a new alias raises the count. Deleting a Ô¨Åle
removes its entry in a directory, reduces its count, and preserves the Ô¨Åle if the
count is not yet zero. If the count is zero, the Ô¨Åle‚Äôs data can be discarded and its
disk space reclaimed. A user who wants to delete a Ô¨Åle completely must know all
its aliases.
¬å
Accounting: Many operating systems charge users in proportion to how much disk
space (in sectors, for example) each user‚Äôs Ô¨Åles are occupying at the end of each
day. Who should pay for the disk space occupied by a Ô¨Åle that has several aliases?
We might charge the ‚Äò‚Äòowner‚Äô‚Äô of the Ô¨Åle, that is, the user who Ô¨Årst created it.
Ownership would be stored with the Ô¨Åle, not with the directory entry, to avoid hav-
ing several owners of the same Ô¨Åle. However, this policy can be unfair. A student,
Patish Shamir, has built the Ô¨Åle project.mod. The instructor has made an alias
for it, called shamir.mod. Patish is paying for the disk space. Now Patish
deletes the Ô¨Åle at the end of the semester. Since the instructor never gets around to
deleting shamir.mod, it remains. Patish is still paying for the Ô¨Åle but has no
way to delete it, since the instructor‚Äôs Ô¨Åles are protected from him. In fact, Patish
may graduate and lose his account on the machine. There is nobody left to bill for
this Ô¨Åle.
A different accounting policy would be to charge each user for the Ô¨Åles in
that user‚Äôs directory in proportion to the percentage of the Ô¨Åle that is in the direc-
tory. For example, a Ô¨Åle with only one name is entirely in its directory. A Ô¨Åle with
two aliases is charged half to one directory and half to the other. Patish Shamir‚Äôs
project Ô¨Åle is charged half to him and half to the instructor until Patish deletes it,
after which it is charged entirely to the instructor. Even this policy is not com-
pletely fair. Before Patish deletes the Ô¨Åle, he might (maliciously) add an enormous
amount of data to it. The instructor is left paying for the whole Ô¨Åle and has no idea
that Patish was so unkind.
If the Ô¨Åle structures allow aliasing, moving a Ô¨Åle from one directory to another can
be very inexpensive and independent of the length of the Ô¨Åle. Instead of copying the Ô¨Åle
to a new name, the new name can be made an alias for the Ô¨Åle, and then the Ô¨Åle can be
deleted under the old name. Since there is still at least one outstanding reference to it, the
Ô¨Åle is not deleted. If aliasing is not allowed, inexpensive Ô¨Åle movement can still be pro-
vided by a service call.
¬ç
Move(old name, new name). This call has the same effect as Alias, except that
it removes the old name. This call is provided only if Alias is not.
BSD4.2 Unix permits another, looser form of aliasing. An indirect Ô¨Åle is a Ô¨Åle
that contains nothing but the name (either absolute or relative) of another Ô¨Åle.
¬é
Indirect(old name, new name). This call builds a new indirect Ô¨Åle for the old
Ô¨Åle.

Figure 6.2 shows an indirect Ô¨Åle,
/troy/smith.text, which refers to the Ô¨Åle
/smith.thesis/chapter1.text. Any attempt to open the indirect Ô¨Åle will result
in opening the other one instead. (Relative names in an indirect Ô¨Åle may be understood
with respect to the working directory of the process or with respect to the directory where
the indirect Ô¨Åle is stored. Unix chooses the second alternative.) A chain of indirect Ô¨Åles
is meaningful, but to prevent getting lost in cycles, the Ô¨Åle manager should refuse to
accept a chain longer than Ô¨Åve or so indirect Ô¨Åles.
Indirect Ô¨Åles raise the same issues of deletion and accounting as aliases.
¬è
Deletion: Deleting an indirect Ô¨Åle A has no effect on the Ô¨Åle B it refers to. Delet-
ing a Ô¨Åle B referred to by an indirect Ô¨Åle A makes A useless. It is then impossible
to open A, but A still remains in its directory. If a Ô¨Åle named B is later created, A
is again usable.
¬ê
Accounting: If an indirect Ô¨Åle A refers to B, the owner of A should pay for the
(minuscule) amount of disk storage A occupies. The owner of B should pay for all
of B. The owner of B can always avoid payment by deleting the Ô¨Åle; this action
renders the indirect Ô¨Åle A worthless. If the owner of A wants to be sure that B
can‚Äôt be deleted, that owner should have to pay for part of B by making a regular
alias.
Aliases and indirect Ô¨Åles form interconnections by means of entries in directory
structures. VMS provides logical names through data structures in the Ô¨Åle manager to
serve a similar purpose. Whenever a relative Ô¨Åle name is presented to the Ô¨Åle manager, it
checks the Ô¨Årst part of the name against several translation tables. The global table
applies to all processes, the group table applies to all processes within a group, and each
process has two private tables, one of which is inherited by its children. System calls are
available to manipulate these tables; modifying the Ô¨Årst two requires special privilege.
Each table entry gives a logical name and its translation. The same logical name may be
translated differently by different tables, and one name may have several translations in
the same table. Each of these translations is tried in a standard order (recursively, since
the translation may itself be a logical name) until a name that corresponds to an actual
Ô¨Åle is found. Logical names can be used to point to directories where standard software
is kept. A user can then refer to
software/editor.run, for example, without
needing to know where the software directory is. People testing new versions can make
a group logical name for software that points to a different directory.
We could have introduced aliases and indirect Ô¨Åles when we were discussing Ô¨Çat
directory structures, but they are much more important when full names become cumber-
some, as they are with two levels. The extension from two levels to an arbitrary number
of levels is the next step.
1.4 Hierarchical directories
It is not hard to generalize the two-level directory structure into an arbitrary number of
layers. We started in that direction when we gave Mary Smith two different accounts,
one for her thesis work and one for her course work. It would have been more helpful to
192
File Structures
Chapter 6

give her one account and let her subdivide her Ô¨Åles between the thesis and course pro-
jects. The course Ô¨Åles might themselves be divided among the various courses she is tak-
ing. At a higher level, the Ô¨Åles on the computer may be divided between those needed by
the operating system (like source Ô¨Åles for the kernel, documentation on the text editor, or
software such as the Fortran compiler) and those needed by users (letters, programs for
projects, documentation, mail).
A tree is an elegant arrangement for these divisions. We distinguish two types of
nodes in the tree: directories and ordinary Ô¨Åles. A directory can have children (sub-
nodes), a non-directory cannot. Directories may be empty. Figure 6.3 shows how the
Ô¨Åles we have been discussing might be placed into a hierarchical structure. As you can
see, Ô¨Åles are not restricted to lie at a particular level in the hierarchy. The top few levels
of the directory structure tend to have a lot of directories, but ordinary Ô¨Åles can reside
there, too.
In a hierarchical directory structure, absolute names are often clumsy. They are
still necessary because we need a way to distinguish a mail Ô¨Åle in Mary Smith‚Äôs directory
from a mail Ô¨Åle in Helen Troy‚Äôs directory. We build the absolute name of a Ô¨Åle by start-
ing at the root of the tree and naming all the directories on the path to that Ô¨Åle. The last
part of the full name is the local name of the Ô¨Åle. We will separate the intermediate
names by the / symbol, as before. The Ô¨Åles in Figure 6.3 have these full names:

What if she wants to copy her slide1.text Ô¨Åle from her courses directory to her
thesis directory? She could submit a command of the following form.
COPY /users/smith/courses/slide1.text
TO /users/smith/thesis/slide1.text
By setting the working directory to /users/smith, she could reduce this command to
the following.
COPY courses/slide1.text
TO thesis/slide1.text
Aliases can also reduce the burden of long names. Files that are heavily used may
have aliases in several directories, so local names usually sufÔ¨Åce. Likewise, indirect Ô¨Åles
may specify shared Ô¨Åles without worrying about accidentally retaining a copy after they
are deleted.
Let us imagine that the command interpreter has an interactive LIST command that
lists the Ô¨Åles in any directory. By grouping related Ô¨Åles, the Ô¨Åle structure makes such a
command both useful and efÔ¨Åcient. Directories seldom grow to hold more than about 20
or so Ô¨Åles. After that point, the user is likely to partition the Ô¨Åles into several subdirec-
tories.
How would we list the contents of the working directory? We could tell the LIST
command the absolute name of the working directory, but absolute names are clumsy.
We could tell LIST the relative name for the working directory, but we have no such
name. We could let LIST assume that if we give it no argument at all, the working
194
File Structures
Chapter 6

directory is implied, but the LIST program would still need to open the working direc-
tory, and it has no name for it. We will therefore adopt a convention that % as a relative
Ô¨Åle name refers to the working directory. Then
LIST %
will list the working directory.
Another nice feature we can add is a relative name that refers to the parent direc-
tory, the one right above the working directory in the tree. We will use ÀÜ for this pur-
pose. If the working directory is courses, then Mary could use the command
COPY slide1.text TO ÀÜ/thesis/slide1.text
.
We might as well generalize the notion of parent to include any number of levels up the
tree. To move the same Ô¨Åle into her advisor‚Äôs account, Mary could submit the following
command.
COPY slide1.text TO ÀÜ/ÀÜ/troy/slide1.text
We see that ÀÜ is a local Ô¨Åle name that refers to the parent directory, and it may be used
in arbitrary ways in a full Ô¨Åle name. Likewise, we treat % as a local Ô¨Åle name in each
directory that refers to that directory. Repeated uses of % in a Ô¨Åle name are not as useful
as repeated uses of ÀÜ.
The easiest way to implement these special Ô¨Åle names is to place these two names
in every directory as aliases to that directory and to its parent. The root directory does
not need a Ô¨Åle ÀÜ.
Setting up a directory is a job for the Ô¨Åle manager because directories must follow
consistency rules to prevent the Ô¨Åle structure from becoming confused. We provide a
service call for this purpose.
¬ë
MakeDirectory(new name). This call builds a new directory with the given
name. The new name may be either absolute or relative to the working directory.
The new directory is initialized to contain the ÀÜ and % Ô¨Åles, which are aliases to
its parent and itself, respectively. This call fails if the preÔ¨Åx of the new name (that
is, all but the last part) is not a directory, if the proposed directory already exists, or
if the caller does not have permission to create this directory.
2 ACCESS CONTROL
Files are used for long-term storage of information. Much information is either private
(and should be secret) or at least important (and should be protected against
modiÔ¨Åcation). Therefore, the rights of users or their processes to access Ô¨Åles must be res-
tricted. Yet Ô¨Åles are an indispensible means of sharing information, so, the restrictions
should be selective.
Restricted access with the possibility of sharing is not a new topic for us. We
encountered it in Chapter 3 when we were discussing main-store management. There
were occasions when two processes would want to share a segment of information. We
will now present a more formal discussion of access control in general.
Access control
195

2.1 A formal model of access control
Our formal model deals with subjects, which are the entities that wish to access data, and
objects, which are the units of data that may be accessed. For our purposes, the subjects
are users (or processes acting on their behalf). Subjects can be represented by user
identiÔ¨Åers, which are associated with all the processes running on behalf of a particular
user. The objects we will deal with are entire Ô¨Åles. We will not deal with access control
on a Ô¨Åner grain than entire Ô¨Åles, although database applications often impose controls on
the record or byte level of granularity. We will allow a process to access either a whole
Ô¨Åle or none of it.
The formal model of subjects and objects can be applied to other situations besides
Ô¨Åle systems. For example, one can describe the scope of identiÔ¨Åers in a Pascal program
by considering procedures as subjects and identiÔ¨Åers as objects. As a second example,
one can describe the interplay of a community of cooperating processes by letting
processes be both subjects and objects.
In each of these subject-object worlds, individual objects may have several access
modes. Files can be read or written. (A more complete list will be presented shortly.)
IdentiÔ¨Åers can be invoked (if they are procedures), read or written (if they are variables),
only read (if they are constants), or applied to new declarations (if they are types).
Cooperating processes can be asked to perform different tasks, depending on the program
that the process is running.
Our formal access model will allow us to specify which access modes are allowed
between each subject and each object. In the Ô¨Åle world, for example, Mary Smith may
not care who looks at her histology notes (mode = read) as long as only she can modify
them (mode = write). But she doesn‚Äôt want anyone else even to look at her thesis, except
possibly her adviser. Her mail Ô¨Åle should be available for people to add messages (mode
= append) but not to overwrite in the middle and certainly not to inspect.
We will therefore determine whether to allow a given access by considering three
factors:
¬í
the subject
¬ì
the object
¬î
the access mode.
To perform an access of a given mode, the subject must have the appropriate access
right. We will use the term privilege as a synonym for ‚Äò‚Äòaccess right.‚Äô‚Äô For Ô¨Åles, the
access modes that might be provided include the following.
¬ï
Read: Derive information from the object.
¬ñ
Write: Initialize or modify the information in the object.
¬ó
Append: Add new information to the object.
¬ò
Execute: Treat the object as a load image.
¬ô
Delete: Remove the object.
¬ö
Privilege: Modify the rights that subjects have to the object.
¬õ
SetOwner: Establish which subject owns the object.
196
File Structures
Chapter 6

Execute privilege is different from Read privilege. One way to distinguish programs
from data Ô¨Åles is by granting Execute privilege over programs. In addition, proprietary
software is often licensed by the manufacturer to be used but not to be copied. In order
to copy a Ô¨Åle, a subject must be able to read it. Separating the Execute and the Read
privileges lets us prohibit copying but not execution.
The Privilege privilege allows a subject to grant new privileges or revoke old
privileges. It is rarely granted to any subject but the owner because it is so powerful.
The SetOwner privilege is even more powerful and may be reserved for administrators.
We can represent the state of all access rights by constructing an access matrix, as
shown in Figure 6.4. Each row in the access matrix represents one subject, and each
column represents one object. The subjects are users, and the objects are Ô¨Åles. The
information stored in the entry for a given row and column is the list of privileges that
subject has over that object. Figure 6.4 only uses the Read, Write, Append, Delete, and
Execute privileges and abbreviates each to one letter.
All the users in Figure 6.4 may read the news Ô¨Åle, and all may execute the editor
Ô¨Åle. Marduk is allowed to read or write any Ô¨Åle. Fred may read, write, or delete his own
mail Ô¨Åle. Many other users may append to that mail Ô¨Åle but not read it. Mottl isn‚Äôt even
allowed to append to Fred‚Äôs mail Ô¨Åle. Ramon and Cheri share letters/love.text.
Ramon may read and write this Ô¨Åle, and Cheri may read it and delete it. Kealoha may
read, write, and delete her prog.text Ô¨Åle, and she has allowed Murali to read it as
well.
The access matrix is purely a logical construct that we can use to reason about the
access control situation. It would be unreasonable to store the matrix in one piece, either
in main store or on secondary store, because it is so large. However, we can divide it up
and store it in pieces.

Whenever a process attempts to access a Ô¨Åle, the Ô¨Åle manager can refer to the
access matrix (however it is stored) to validate the access. In keeping with the Cache
Principle, the Ô¨Åle manager might check for privileges only when the Ô¨Åle is opened. We

when a process uses the Open service call to access a Ô¨Åle, it indicates the sort of access it
intends. At this point the Ô¨Åle manager checks that the access meets the restrictions in the
access matrix. After the Ô¨Åle is opened, the only check that needs to be made on every
access is that the access matches the declared intent. One effect of this design is that
modiÔ¨Åcations to the access matrix while Ô¨Åles are open will not affect processes that
already have the Ô¨Åle open.

One simple form of access list is to associate one or more passwords with each Ô¨Åle.

write passwords to restrict read and write access to Ô¨Åles. Files without passwords are
public.
2.4 Directories and access control

allow greater use of the default mechanism outlined previously. We will adopt the fol-
lowing access rule.

Directories have somewhat different operations from ordinary Ô¨Åles. We could
invent a new set of permission types for directories, but for the sake of uniformity, we
will use the same permissions that we use for ordinary Ô¨Åles and give them slightly dif-
ferent meanings:
¬®
Read: Determine the names of the Ô¨Åles in the directory.
¬©

open the directory for writing. The only way to modify the directory is by service
calls.
¬™

¬´
Execute: Open Ô¨Åles in this directory.
¬¨

¬≠

¬Æ

Both Delete privilege for a Ô¨Åle and Write privilege for its directory allow a subject to
delete that Ô¨Åle. The operating system can be designed without a Delete privilege, or the
Ô¨Åle manager can require that a subject hold both privileges before allowing it to delete a
Ô¨Åle. A process with Read but not Execute privilege over a directory can determine what
Ô¨Åles are in the directory but cannot open any of those Ô¨Åles. A process with Execute but
not Read privilege can open Ô¨Åles in the directory if it knows their names, but it cannot
discover their names by reading the directory. If this subtle distinction is not important,

we mentioned earlier, this check might be performed only when the process Ô¨Årst opens
the Ô¨Åle. If permissions are later restricted somewhere in that path, the process is not
prevented from continuing the access it is making.

2.5 Aliases and indirect Ô¨Åles
The accessibility of a Ô¨Åle depends on what the Ô¨Åle is called, as we have just seen. All the
intermediate Ô¨Åles between the working directory (for relative names) or the root (for
absolute names) must be accessible. If a single Ô¨Åle has several aliases, they may differ in
accessibility, since the intermediate Ô¨Åles may be different. However, after all the inter-
mediate directories have been successfully passed, the subject must still have the neces-
sary privileges over the Ô¨Åle itself.
If all directories grant Execute privilege, can a subject be granted access to some
Ô¨Åle but denied access to the same Ô¨Åle under a different alias? The answer depends on
whether privileges are associated with a Ô¨Åle or with a name. If they are associated with
the Ô¨Åle, they are not stored in the directory (since several directory entries in several
directories may all refer to the same Ô¨Åle). In this case, the alias chosen for the Ô¨Åle does
not inÔ¨Çuence its accessibility. This is the design choice favored by Unix. If privileges
are associated with a name, they might as well be stored in the directory. In this case, the
alias chosen can determine accessibility.
Indirect Ô¨Åles are different.

would require Execute privilege on indirect Ô¨Åle 7 and directories 1, 2, 3, and 4 and Write
privilege on Ô¨Åle 5. Sometimes the privileges on indirect Ô¨Åles are taken as ordinary-Ô¨Åle
privileges, and sometimes they are taken as directory privileges. Perhaps it is simpler,
after all, to ignore them. BSD4.2 Unix takes this approach. It also understands the
ChangePrivileges call to apply not to the indirect Ô¨Åle it is called on but rather to the Ô¨Åle
that the indirect Ô¨Åle refers to.
3 ACCESS METHODS
Processes read and write Ô¨Åles. Specifying which part of the Ô¨Åle is to be read or written
into depends on the access method for that Ô¨Åle. The access method dictates both which
location in the Ô¨Åle and how much data at that location are to be accessed. The access
method might be speciÔ¨Åed at the following times:
¬≥
When the operating system is designed. In this case, all Ô¨Åles use the same access
method.
¬¥
When the Ô¨Åle is created. In this case, every time the Ô¨Åle is opened, the same access
method will be used.
¬µ
When the Ô¨Åle is opened. Several processes can have the same Ô¨Åle open and access
it differently.
Let us look at some of the more popular access methods.
3.1 Sequential access
Many applications require only sequential access, in which the entire Ô¨Åle is read or writ-
ten from the beginning to the end. Each operation implicitly advances a pointer that indi-
cates where the next operation should take place. We call this pointer the read/write
mark. Occasionally, separate read and write marks are kept for the same open Ô¨Åle. (An
Access methods
205

early version of the Ada programming language separated these marks, but the current
design does not.) If several processes have the same Ô¨Åle open for reading, each has its
own read mark; operations undertaken by one process do not affect the behavior of the
other one.
The read/write mark of an open Ô¨Åle is therefore part of the context block of a pro-
cess and is not part of the Ô¨Åle itself. Context blocks can be designed to contain an array
of open-Ô¨Åle records. (The same array can be used for open-device descriptors.) Each
record contains information describing the Ô¨Åle that is open, the intent of the process
(whether to read, write, and so on), the read/write mark, and information about where the
Ô¨Åle is stored on the physical disk. The index of a particular open-Ô¨Åle record is a con-
venient number to return to the process as the result of the Open service call. The pro-
cess can use this Ô¨Åle number in its subsequent requests regarding the open Ô¨Åle.
Sequential access might allow each operation to transfer an arbitrary number of
bytes, or there might be a Ô¨Åxed-size increment (a block) that must be transferred on each
operation. Fixed-size increments can be a characteristic of the installation, the Ô¨Åle, or the
instance of the open Ô¨Åle. If the increment is Ô¨Åxed, the read/write mark may be expressed
by the number of full increments from the start of the Ô¨Åle.
3.2 Direct access
Some applications need to move the read/write mark to speciÔ¨Åc places without reading or
writing the intervening parts of the Ô¨Åle. They might even want to move the read/write
mark backward in the Ô¨Åle. For example, a program that traverses a large data tree stored
in a Ô¨Åle might need to move to an arbitrary place in the Ô¨Åle to Ô¨Ånd the next node of the
tree. We can use a Position service call for Ô¨Åles just as we did in Chapter 5 for devices.
¬∂
Position(Ô¨Åle number, where). This call tells the Ô¨Åle manager to position the
read/write mark in the Ô¨Åle represented by the given Ô¨Åle number. The ‚Äò‚Äòwhere‚Äô‚Äô
parameter might be speciÔ¨Åed by its location from the start of the Ô¨Åle, the end of the
Ô¨Åle, or the current location of the read/write mark. The distance from that location
is given as a number of Ô¨Åxed-size increments (perhaps bytes).
3.3 Mapped access
The Tenex and Multics operating systems provide a novel form of Ô¨Åle access called
mapped access. When a process opens a Ô¨Åle, the entire Ô¨Åle is mapped into the virtual
space of the process. Instead of returning a Ô¨Åle number, the Open service call returns a
segment number. This number refers to a new segment that contains only the Ô¨Åle. Simi-
larly, the Dynix operating system for the Sequent Balance computer allows Ô¨Åles that have
already been opened to be mapped into virtual store.
206
File Structures
Chapter 6

Instead of submitting subsequent requests to read or write, the process treats the
Ô¨Åle as part of virtual store. To close the Ô¨Åle, the process needs only to submit a
ReleaseSegment service call.
None of the Ô¨Åle itself needs to be brought into main store at Ô¨Årst. Instead, as the
process accesses the new segment, it will generate address-translation faults. The storage
manager will convert these faults into requests on the Ô¨Åle manager to bring in more of the
Ô¨Åle.
3.4 Structured Ô¨Åles
Direct access requires the application program to compute where it wishes to seek in the
Ô¨Åle. This computation may be based on data stored elsewhere in the Ô¨Åle, or it may be
based on some secondary Ô¨Åle that maintains indices into the main Ô¨Åle. Database applica-
tions, which we will discuss shortly, often wish to seek to the part in the Ô¨Åle that
corresponds to some record, as identiÔ¨Åed by the value of some key Ô¨Åeld in the record.
For example, a Ô¨Åle might contain data about all the students at a university. Each
student‚Äôs data could be stored in a single record, with Ô¨Åelds for name, year in school,
grade-point average, mailing address, and a list of all the courses the student has taken.
An application program might wish to examine records only of second-year students. In
this case, the year-in-school Ô¨Åeld is a key to the Ô¨Åle. Another application might wish to
examine records of students with straight A averages. Here, the running grade-point
average Ô¨Åeld is treated as a key.
A few operating systems allow the owner of a Ô¨Åle to declare that the Ô¨Åle should be
structured. Part of structuring a Ô¨Åle is declaring what the records look like. In particu-
lar, the Ô¨Åelds that will be used as keys must be described. Writing into the Ô¨Åle is limited
to placing new records into the Ô¨Åle and modifying or deleting old ones. Reading the Ô¨Åle
requires specifying the keys and key values that the Ô¨Åle manager should search for. Suc-
cessive read operations yield successive records that satisfy the current search pattern.
The records themselves might not have any particular order. OS/360 (and its descen-
dants) provides structured Ô¨Åles of several kinds.
One example is ISAM (Indexed
Sequential Access Method), which uses a two-level tree to access records indexed by a
single key. VMS provides a record-management tool that includes a language for
describing the structure of Ô¨Åles and a set of routines that programs can use to access the
structured Ô¨Åles.
3.5 Databases
A database is a collection of structured, interrelated data, usually managed by utility pro-
grams that perform queries and updates. These programs, which are called database
management systems, provide for complex searches that may involve a number of related
Ô¨Åles. For example, Ô¨Ånding the home phone number of all students who are majoring in
computer science and have a grade point average of at least 3.5 may require reference to
Access methods
207

a student Ô¨Åle, a majors Ô¨Åle, and a grade Ô¨Åle. The exact details depend on the way the
database is structured, which must, of course, be known to the database management sys-
tem.
Large-scale database management systems often Ô¨Ånd themselves in conÔ¨Çict with
the policies forced upon them by the underlying operating system. These conÔ¨Çicts
include physical disk allocation strategies, kernel caching (in particular, write-behind),
and main-store management.
4 FILE RECOVERY
Every user at some point accidentally deletes a Ô¨Åle and thereby destroys many hours of
diligent labor. It does not do much good to consider that repeating the labor will be much
easier than doing it the Ô¨Årst time and that the product will be better after this rework. The
operating system can help reconstruct the lost Ô¨Åle if necessary measures have been taken.
Since recovering a lost Ô¨Åle is a form of reliability, it is no surprise that all the techniques
that the operating system might use involve redundancy of some form or another.
4.1 Dumps
Almost all operating systems provide for a fairly large grain of redundancy: Every so
often, an archival copy (a dump) of Ô¨Åles is made on magnetic tape. The unfortunate user
who has lost a Ô¨Åle to accidental deletion can recover it by Ô¨Ånding the most recent version
that is stored on tape. Since storing all the Ô¨Åles that exist might take many reels of tape, it
is not reasonable to make complete archives very often. However, infrequent archives
increase the chance that an archival copy of the deleted Ô¨Åle, even if one exists, is far out
of date.
Instead, partial Ô¨Åle dumps are commonly made every day. These tapes include
only Ô¨Åles that have been modiÔ¨Åed since the last partial backup. The user who acciden-
tally deletes a Ô¨Åle can recover at least yesterday‚Äôs version, which is often better than
nothing at all. If the Ô¨Åle has not been modiÔ¨Åed for a few days, it can be found in a more
ancient partial dump. Partial dump tapes may be reused after a few weeks; if so much
time has elapsed since the Ô¨Åle was last modiÔ¨Åed, it is likely to be on a full dump tape,
which is saved for a much longer time.
Instead of two classes of dump, one-day and total, we can devise a general scheme
that tries to minimize the number of tapes needed but maximize the chances that we have
saved the Ô¨Åle somewhere. Let‚Äôs assume that we have eight tapes. To keep things simple,
we will pretend that a single tape is always long enough to hold all the Ô¨Åles we dump
when we use that tape. (In reality, tapes 0 and 1 can be fairly short, but tapes 6 and 7 will
have to be quite long.) Number all the days that the operating system runs, starting with
day 1. On day n , dump Ô¨Åles on tape t , where 2t is the highest power of 2 that divides n .
For example, on day 51, we use tape 0, but on day 52, we use tape 2. On day 64, we use
208
File Structures
Chapter 6

tape 6. Whenever we use tape t , we dump all Ô¨Åles that were modiÔ¨Åed since the last time
we used tape t or any tape with a higher number.
We use the lower-numbered tapes fairly frequently, so they don‚Äôt have to store
very many Ô¨Åles. The higher-numbered tapes are used much more rarely, so they hold
many more Ô¨Åles. Tape 7 is written only every 128 days. If we wish to archive Ô¨Åles
longer than that, we just need to buy some more tapes.
This pattern of tape use is known as the ‚Äò‚Äòruler function‚Äô‚Äô because its graph looks
like the pattern of marks on a ruler. Figure 6.9 shows which tape we use each day by
drawing a vertical bar whose height tells the number of the tape to use. If a Ô¨Åle is created
on day c and deleted on day d , a dump version will be saved substantially after day d ,
and the length of time it is saved depends on d ‚àíc (as well as the exact values of c and
d ). For example, a Ô¨Åle created on day 5 and deleted on day 8 has existed for 3 days.
During that time, it was saved on tapes 0 (day 5), 1 (day 6), and 0 (day 7). Tape 1 will
not be reused until day 10, so the Ô¨Åle is saved 2 extra days. A 3-day Ô¨Åle created on day 6
and deleted on day 9 has been saved on tape 3 (day 8), which will not be reused until day
24.
4.2 Backup Ô¨Åles and versions
Most computer installations hire operators to dump tapes according to a Ô¨Åxed schedule;
these dumps form the last defense against lost information. There are other defenses that
are worth mentioning. First, backup Ô¨Åles that duplicate working Ô¨Åles are a standard
technique. If the user (or the user‚Äôs program) accidentally deletes a working Ô¨Åle, its
backup can still be used. Some programs (particularly text editors) automatically save an
old version of the Ô¨Åle they are working on under a name derived from the Ô¨Åle‚Äôs name.
For example, if the editor is working on Ô¨Åle slide1.text, it might save the original
contents under the name slide1.text.backup.
Backup Ô¨Åles can be generalized to Ô¨Åle versions. Whenever a Ô¨Åle is opened for
writing, a new version is created that is initialized to the data in the previous version of
the Ô¨Åle. The new version is opened, and the old version is left alone. ModiÔ¨Åcations to
this version do not hurt the previous version. A wide spectrum of support is available for
Figure 6.9 The ruler function
File recovery
209

Ô¨Åle versions on different operating systems.
¬∑
Manual: A user who is about to modify a Ô¨Åle Ô¨Årst copies it to a new name and
modiÔ¨Åes the Ô¨Åle under the new name.
¬∏
Special utility: Some programs, particularly the text editor, copy the Ô¨Åle they are
about to modify to a new name and modify the new Ô¨Åle.
¬π
General utility: When requested, a general utility program copies the Ô¨Åle to a new
name. A variant of this is a librarian program that allows Ô¨Åles to be checked in and
out. Checking in a Ô¨Åle creates a new version of the Ô¨Åle. Some librarian programs
save space by storing only differences between successive versions. It is possible,
although perhaps time consuming, to recover any version.
¬∫
Automatic: When a process opens a Ô¨Åle for writing, the storage manager opens a
fresh copy of that Ô¨Åle. The version number is part of the Ô¨Åle name. If it is not
mentioned when the Ô¨Åle is opened, the most recent version is assumed. Tenex fol-
lows this policy. Exec-8 is similar, but only a limited number of version numbers
are available. Every time a new version is started, if the maximum number of ver-
sions are already stored for the Ô¨Åle, the oldest one is overwritten. Files under VMS
can have up to about 32K versions, although the number can be restricted on a
directory or Ô¨Åle basis.
All these mechanisms provide for deleting the most recent version (usually the default
deletion operation), a particular version, a set of versions, or the entire Ô¨Åle.
4.3 Partial deletion
Another defense mechanism is partial deletion of Ô¨Åles. The DeleteFile service call
could mark the Ô¨Åle as deleted without reclaiming its storage on the disk. If the user later
decides that the Ô¨Åle should not have been deleted, an UndeleteFile service call could
change the mark and place the Ô¨Åle back into full service. Partially deleted Ô¨Åles might be
completely deleted by a PurgeFile service call. Alternatively, they could be purged when
the user‚Äôs session ends or when space is needed for other Ô¨Åles. If the operating system
does not provide direct support for partially deleted Ô¨Åles, the same effect can sometimes
be implemented by letting the ‚Äò‚Äòdelete Ô¨Åle‚Äô‚Äô interactive command move the Ô¨Åle to a direc-
tory reserved for partially deleted Ô¨Åles.
One variant of the partial-deletion scheme is to structure Ô¨Åles on the disk in such a
way that even though Ô¨Åle deletion frees disk space, the Ô¨Åle may still be reconstructed
until that space is used, although possibly by a time-consuming process. A ‚Äò‚Äòrecover
Ô¨Åle‚Äô‚Äô program, which the user invokes in an emergency, tries to reconstruct the Ô¨Åle if pos-
sible from the remnants that still reside on the disk. We will see how physical Ô¨Åle struc-
tures can be chosen to meet this need.
At the other extreme, VMS allows the user to specify that all the disk blocks con-
taining a Ô¨Åle be erased when it is destroyed, to prevent others from viewing sensitive
data.

5 TRANSACTIONS
We have been concentrating on Ô¨Åles that are used one at a time by one user at a time.
Both of these biases are inappropriate for databases, where single operations might
require access to several Ô¨Åles, and different users may undertake simultaneous operations
that need to access the same Ô¨Åle. To prevent chaos, we need to ensure that these
independent operations start with all Ô¨Åles in a consistent state and leave them in a con-
sistent state.
The problem is similar to allocating sequentially reusable, non-preemptable
resources, discussed in Chapter 4. There are differences, however. We would like to
allow several processes to have the same Ô¨Åle open for reading as long as none of the
processes modiÔ¨Åes the Ô¨Åle. File resources are sharable up to a point, unlike the ones we
saw in Chapter 4. In addition, we may need to refer to several Ô¨Åles in order. If another
process changes some of the later Ô¨Åles after we have read the Ô¨Årst Ô¨Åles, we might think
that the Ô¨Åles are inconsistent.
We will use the term transaction to refer to a set of related Ô¨Åle accesses, possibly
to a number of Ô¨Åles, that one process undertakes in order to perform its work. One pro-
cess may be involved in several independent transactions at one time, but this situation is
unusual. It is more common for a process to Ô¨Ånish one transaction before starting the
next one. Our problem is to make sure that transactions do not interfere with each other.
5.1 Synchronization atomicity
Our Ô¨Årst requirement is that transactions be serializable:

The simplest form of synchronization atomicity applies to transactions on only one Ô¨Åle.
If process A is modifying a Ô¨Åle F in several places, but process B is reading F, we would
like B‚Äôs view of F either to omit any of A‚Äôs modiÔ¨Åcations or to include them all. In most
operating systems, this result is achieved by preventing A and B from opening the Ô¨Åle at
the same time. This policy enforces strict serialization on accesses to the same Ô¨Åle.
Not all simultaneous Ô¨Åle accesses lead to failures of synchronization atomicity.
There is no problem with any number of processes opening the Ô¨Åle for reading. The
problem occurs only if one process wishes to write when some other process wants to
read or write. This set of constraints is commonly known as the readers-writers prob-

Many operating systems prevent any process from opening a Ô¨Åle that is already
open for writing (by any process). A Ô¨Åle open for reading may not be opened for writing.
This policy prevents any of the conÔ¨Çicts of the readers-writers problem. If the Ô¨Åle
manager does not provide this service, it can be awkward or impossible to build it out of
other services that are provided.
In BSD4.2 Unix, Ô¨Åle interlock is optional. Processes can open Ô¨Åles requesting
either exclusive or shared access to the Ô¨Åle. Shared access is intended for processes that
only want to read the Ô¨Åle. Exclusive access is for those that want to modify the Ô¨Åle.
While a Ô¨Åle is open with exclusive access, no process is allowed to open it with shared or
exclusive access. While a Ô¨Åle is open with shared access, no process is allowed to open it
with exclusive access. However, a process may violate the rules by opening the Ô¨Åle
without specifying either shared or exclusive access. In VMS, individual records of
structured Ô¨Åles may be locked separately.
A transaction may involve several Ô¨Åles. Most operating systems do not provide
synchronization atomicity in such cases. However, we can provide this feature for data-
base utilities that need it. Instead of forcing the Ô¨Åle manager to undertake this task, we
will speak of a transaction manager in the kernel that handles complex cases.
To provide synchronization atomicity in such cases, the transaction manager must
know when a transaction starts and when it Ô¨Ånishes. Every open Ô¨Åle may be associated
with some transaction. We could automatically start a transaction every time a user starts
a session and associate all Ô¨Åle actions during that session with the same transaction.
However, this policy would lead to needlessly long transactions that would interfere with
other users. Instead, it is better to let a process open a transaction, much as it might open
212
File Structures
Chapter 6

a Ô¨Åle, and to specify the transaction each time it opens a Ô¨Åle. Here are the service calls
involved:
√à
StartTransaction. This call tells the transaction manager that a transaction is
starting. The call returns a transaction number for the process to use.
√â
EndTransaction(transaction number). This call tells the transaction manager
that the transaction has Ô¨Ånished. It is illegal to end a transaction for which some
Ô¨Åles are still open.
√ä

returns a Ô¨Åle number. The ‚Äò‚Äòintent‚Äô‚Äô parameter indicates whether the Ô¨Åle is to be
read, written, or appended. The special transaction number OneShot means that
the transaction involves only this one Ô¨Åle. The special transaction number NoTran-
saction means that the process wishes to violate transaction rules for this Ô¨Åle.
5.2 Failure atomicity
As we pointed out earlier, transactions are particularly useful for processes that deal with
databases. During the middle of a transaction, when some Ô¨Åle modiÔ¨Åcations have been
made but others have not been made yet, the database is likely to be inconsistent. For
example, transferring funds from one bank account to another might require two separate
write accesses, one for each account. Synchronization atomicity ensures that no other
transaction will see the two accounts in an inconsistent state, with money deducted from
one but not yet added to the other or added to one without being deducted from the other.
However, a hardware or software failure between the two accesses might force the tran-
saction to terminate with the database in such an inconsistent state. Similarly, the tran-
saction manager may need to abort a transaction that enters a deadlock as it opens a Ô¨Åle.
(We will see why later when we discuss implementing transactions.) At this time, the
database may be inconsistent.
Most database management systems require that such inconsistencies be prevented.
Failure atomicity is deÔ¨Åned as follows:

Each transaction is guaranteed either to complete
or to have no effect whatsoever.

A failure in the middle of a transaction should either cause all modiÔ¨Åcations undertaken
by the transaction so far to be undone or should preserve the state of the Ô¨Åles so that the
transaction will be completed once the failure has been repaired.
Most operating systems do not address either synchronization or failure atomicity
except to forbid simultaneous multiple accesses to the same Ô¨Åle. (Unix does not even go
that far.) However, the increasing importance of database management systems is lead-
ing to more sophisticated transaction support within operating systems. Another aspect
of transactions, permanence, is a goal of most Ô¨Åle managers: Once a transaction
Transactions
213

completes successfully, the results of its operations will never be lost.
6 PHYSICAL REPRESENTATION
There are many ways to organize Ô¨Åles on disks. A few principles seem to be universal.
√è
Disk blocks have numbers, and complex structures can be placed on the disk by
having data in one block refer to another block by number.
√ê
Each Ô¨Åle is described by a Ô¨Åle descriptor, which tells how the Ô¨Åle is physically
arranged on the disk.
√ë
Each physical disk is described by a disk descriptor, which tells how the disk is
arranged into areas and which parts are currently unused. The disk descriptor is
stored at a well known location on the disk.
√í
Information may be stored redundantly on the disk to allow programs to try to res-
tructure the disk if it gets confused. Confusion is the typical result of unscheduled
operating-system failures, because the structure may be undergoing modiÔ¨Åcation at
the time of the failure. Even worse, the disk may be in the middle of writing a
block when failure occurs. Restructuring a garbaged disk is called salvaging.
√ì
The basic unit of allocation is the single disk block, although entire tracks or
cylinders may be allocated at once to keep large regions in a Ô¨Åle contiguous on the
disk. This attempt to keep Ô¨Åles in local regions on the disk is called clustering. It
is based on the Cache Principle, since it is faster to read or write on the disk at
cylinders close to the current position, and the most likely request to come after
one Ô¨Åle request is another request on the same Ô¨Åle. Clustering may also be
attempted to keep Ô¨Åles that are in the same directory positioned close together on
the disk. Another form of clustering, called skewing, spaces consecutive blocks of
a Ô¨Åle a few sectors apart. As a result, a typical process reading the entire Ô¨Åle will
Ô¨Ånd the next Ô¨Åle block under the disk read/write head at the time it needs it. Some
disk controllers interleave sectors to place consecutively numbered ones some dis-
tance from each other on the same track. In this case, the Ô¨Åle manager should most
likely not attempt skewing.
√î
Searching Ô¨Åle structures and allocating free blocks would be too time consuming if
the information is stored only on the disk. In accordance with the Cache Principle,
some structure and allocation information is duplicated in main store. As is typi-
cally the case with caches, the cached (main-store) data and the actual (disk) data
will be out of step. Operating system failures (crashes) are therefore even more
serious than they seem because they may lose recent changes. To mitigate the
danger, all main-store caches are occasionally (perhaps every minute) archived to
the disk. Perhaps the worst time for a catastrophic failure is during archiving.
√ï
The facilities provided by the Ô¨Åle service determine the structures that must be
used. For example, direct access into arbitrary positions in a Ô¨Åle requires different
structures from sequential access.
Hierarchical directories and Ô¨Çat directories
require different structures. Different methods of access control also need dif-
ferent structures.
214
File Structures
Chapter 6

6.1 Allocation
We will Ô¨Årst examine the way that free space on the disk may be allocated. If we know
the size of the Ô¨Åle and wish to maximize clustering, we may allocate entire tracks or
cylinders to the Ô¨Åle. To make this allocation efÔ¨Åcient, bit maps of free cylinders and
tracks may be kept for each disk. These maps are kept on the disk, but a copy is also
kept in a main-store cache.
A small example of cylinder and track bit maps is shown in Figure 6.10. Each
track is represented in both maps. The maps are arranged in the order of blocks on the
disk so that contiguous bits refer to contiguous tracks (that is, tracks on the same or adja-
cent cylinders). Figure 6.10 shades tracks that are in use. In this Ô¨Ågure, there are eight
cylinders, numbered 0 to 7. (Typical disk packs have 200.) Each cylinder has four
tracks.

When a Ô¨Åle is to be created, if it is large enough to require at least one cylinder, it
may be given a number of entire cylinders from the free-cylinder bit map. We would use
cylinder 0 for a one-cylinder Ô¨Åle. A two-cylinder Ô¨Åle could Ô¨Åt either in cylinders 2 and 3
or in cylinders 6 and 7. Any larger Ô¨Åle will have to be satisÔ¨Åed with non-contiguous
cylinders.
If a Ô¨Åle requires less than a cylinder, we will measure its requirements in tracks. A
Ô¨Åle that needs only one track can Ô¨Åt in cylinder 1, either track 1 or 3, or in cylinder 5,
track 3. A two-track Ô¨Åle can be placed in cylinder 5, tracks 0 and 1. If there are not
enough contiguous tracks, cylinders may be converted to tracks. For a three-track Ô¨Åle,
we can allocate cylinder 0 and mark it in use. We could then use its tracks 0, 1, and 2,
and leave 3 marked as available.
When a Ô¨Åle is deleted and its space becomes available, the tracks it used are made
available in the track map. If all the tracks in a cylinder become free, that cylinder is
marked available in the cylinder map, and all the tracks are marked in use.
Allocating a Ô¨Åle in a single contiguous region has several advantages. The direc-
tory entry (which must also be stored on the disk) needs to indicate only the starting point
and the length of the Ô¨Åle. Direct access in the Ô¨Åle is easy because the Ô¨Åle manager can
compute the block number of any byte in the Ô¨Åle. Clustering within Ô¨Åles is perfect.
Unfortunately, this method produces substantial internal waste, because most Ô¨Åles
need far less than a single track, and even large Ô¨Åles are likely to waste about half of the
last track (or cylinder) allocated to them. Also, it depends on knowledge of the size of
the Ô¨Åle before the allocation is made. In many situations the size can be estimated only
roughly. This deÔ¨Åciency leads to internal waste in some cases and inability to Ô¨Åt the Ô¨Åle
in other cases. If the Ô¨Åle doesn‚Äôt Ô¨Åt, it can be broken into individually contiguous pieces
called extents. Within an extent, sequential and direct access are very fast, but the Ô¨Åle
manager needs to record where each extent is. That information must be read before any
extent can be found, so access is slower than it would be with a single contiguous region.
OS/360 allocates contiguous regions in integral numbers of cylinders or tracks and allows
up to 15 extents.
External waste can also be a problem with contiguous allocation. When the disk
becomes relatively full, it may be impossible to create a new Ô¨Åle because there are not
enough contiguous tracks, even though the total amount of free space is sufÔ¨Åcient.
ShufÔ¨Çing the Ô¨Åles is possible but usually requires that the entire disk be copied to a stag-
ing area (another disk or magnetic tape) and then copied back.
At the other extreme, a Ô¨Åle manager can be designed to allocate all Ô¨Åles by single
blocks. If a Ô¨Åle needs many blocks, they may end up scattered across the disk; we will
see how this dispersion can be controlled. Free-storage allocation can again make use of
a bit map. The map would have a bit for each block on the disk instead of each track or
each cylinder. However, this bit map might be unacceptably large. A medium-large disk
has about 256M bytes. If each block contains 512 bytes, the disk has 512K blocks. The
bit map occupies 64K bytes. We could cache part of the bit map in main store and keep
the full map on the disk, where it would use 128 blocks.
One nice trick is to place parts of the map in the free blocks themselves. Instead of
allocating a Ô¨Åxed region of 128 blocks for the bit map, a single block is allocated. This
block does not hold a bit map but rather stores the numbers of up to 256 free blocks.
(We assume that a block number occupies two bytes.) Each of these free blocks may
store numbers of other free blocks. Thus free space is organized as a tree with a very
large fan-out. The exercises at the end of this chapter develop this idea further.

The FileType Ô¨Åeld (line 5) is used to distinguish major categories of Ô¨Åles. Directories are
used in a very different way from data Ô¨Åles. Device Ô¨Åles are usually not Ô¨Åles at all but a
convenient way to unify transput with the Ô¨Åle system. On some operating systems, such
as Exec-8 for the Univac 1100 computer, the Data type is further categorized as ‚Äò‚Äòsource
program,‚Äô‚Äô ‚Äò‚Äòobject code,‚Äô‚Äô or ‚Äò‚Äòload image.‚Äô‚Äô One can imagine other categories as well,
such as ‚Äò‚Äòmail Ô¨Åle‚Äô‚Äô and ‚Äò‚Äòcommand Ô¨Åle.‚Äô‚Äô In those operating systems that do not use the
Ô¨Åle descriptor to distinguish these different uses for data Ô¨Åles, they can still be dis-
tinguished by the Ô¨Åle name (using conventions we discussed earlier) or by the Ô¨Åle con-
tents.
The Layout Ô¨Åeld (line 11) is used to Ô¨Ånd the physical block that holds any given
byte of the Ô¨Åle. For the purpose of sequential access, each physical block can also con-
tain the block number of its successor. This extra information can reduce the number of
Seek operations needed to read an entire Ô¨Åle sequentially. Of course, such redundant
information must be updated when the contents of the Ô¨Åle change (for example, when
new data are written at the end). If this redundant information (which may be treated as a
hint) is wrong, the truth (that is, the corresponding absolute) may be deduced from the
Ô¨Åle descriptor. At the worst, a salvage operation may be able to reconstruct the Ô¨Åle from
extra information stored in each data block identifying the Ô¨Åle to which it belongs.
The declarations above are intended for Ô¨Åle structures that allow aliases. Each
alias for the Ô¨Åle has its own directory entry, and all those entries point to the same Ô¨Åle
Physical representation
217

descriptor (line 22). This pointer is most likely in the form of an index into the Ô¨Åle
descriptor array. If we forbid aliasing of Ô¨Åles, we don‚Äôt need the reference count (line
10), and the directory entry for each Ô¨Åle can include the Ô¨Åle descriptor instead of pointing
to it. That is, line 22 would read as follows.
22
Descriptor : FileDescriptorType;
The reference count (line 10) is maintained by the Ô¨Åle manager so that it can
reclaim the Ô¨Åle descriptor, along with the data blocks in the Ô¨Åle, when the last alias for
the Ô¨Åle is removed.
Multiple versions of a Ô¨Åle require the Version Ô¨Åeld (line 21). Each version of a Ô¨Åle
is listed separately in the directory with its own Ô¨Åle descriptor or pointer to a Ô¨Åle descrip-
tor. If two versions are almost identical, space can be saved on the disk by sharing some
Ô¨Åle blocks. Whenever the Ô¨Åle is opened for writing, a new version can be constructed
with a new directory entry.
Initially, the Ô¨Åle descriptor for the new version is an exact copy of the one for the
previous version. Both the previous and the new version have the same Layout Ô¨Åeld (line
11), so they share disk blocks. Whenever a modiÔ¨Åcation is made to a block in the new
version, the entire block is copied, and the descriptor of the new version is updated to
show the location of the new block. That is, blocks of the Ô¨Åle are split into separate
copies only when necessary. Special care is needed to avoid splitting when unnecessary
and to postpone reclaiming a disk block until all the versions that use it have been
deleted. We saw a similar copy on write strategy earlier when we were discussing seg-
mentation.
To save more disk space, a utility program can detect similarities by comparing
versions on a block-by-block basis and discarding redundant blocks as they are found.
This operation could be carried out whenever a version is closed. Since it is not critical,
it can be performed ofÔ¨Çine, that is, whenever the opportunity arises, perhaps late at night.
Line 24 in the declarations above suggests that a directory is a linear array of direc-
tory entries. This array can be searched sequentially as long as the directory is not too
long. When a Ô¨Åle is deleted, its slot in the array can be freed (for example, by putting a
special value in the Descriptor Ô¨Åeld, line 22) to be used by the next Ô¨Åle that is created. If
directories are very long, as can happen with Ô¨Çat directories, a hashing or tree-based
technique may well be better for organizing the entries in the array.
To allow the disk to be salvaged, it is important that Ô¨Åle descriptors be distinguish-
able from data blocks and from free blocks. A byte at the start of each block could be
reserved to indicate the nature of the block, but that would reduce the amount of space
available for data in each data block. That extra byte can get in the way if the disk driver
needs to read several blocks into an extended main-store buffer. Alternatively, a region
of the disk could be reserved for Ô¨Åle descriptors. We could call this region the descrip-
tor area.
To promote clustering, the entire disk can be subdivided into subdisks, each com-
posed of a number of consecutive cylinders. Each subdisk has its descriptor area. The
Ô¨Åles described by those descriptors use data blocks preferably within the same region. A
separate free-block structure would be appropriate for each subdisk.
If we want Ô¨Åle descriptors to be recognizable, it is wise to make them all the same
size no matter how large the Ô¨Åle they represent. In that way the salvaging operation can
be sure of the location of all Ô¨Åle descriptors. Some of the information we might store in
the Ô¨Åle descriptor has a constant size: access rights (assuming that we divide the users
into a Ô¨Åxed number of well-deÔ¨Åned groups), creation time, time of last backup, expiration
218
File Structures
Chapter 6

date (for automatic deletion), number of aliases (for reclamation after deletion from a
directory), and Ô¨Åle type. In contrast, pointers to the blocks that hold the Ô¨Åle contents
might require any amount of space. For the following discussion, we will assume that
the Ô¨Åle is constructed in extents, each of which comprises a number of contiguous blocks.
We need to store the length and starting block for each extent.
One organization is to place length and starting-block information for the Ô¨Årst
extent in the Ô¨Åle descriptor and to reserve space in each extent to describe the next one.
This linked organization is Ô¨Åne for sequential access but clumsy for direct access.
Alternatively, the Ô¨Åle descriptor could contain information about every extent.
However, it would be wasteful to allocate enough overhead space in each Ô¨Åle descriptor
for the largest possible Ô¨Åle. Instead, a Ô¨Åxed number of pointers may be stored and an
indirect scheme used if the Ô¨Åle is larger. Most Ô¨Åles are very short. (A recent study at the
University of Wisconsin showed that about 42 percent of all Ô¨Åles on a Unix installation
for a research community Ô¨Åt within one block, and 73 percent Ô¨Åt within Ô¨Åve blocks. A
similar study at Los Alamos ScientiÔ¨Åc Laboratory showed that half the Ô¨Åles were smaller
than 40K bytes, whereas many Ô¨Åles reached 10M bytes or larger.)
We might therefore
limit the storage in the Ô¨Åle descriptor to describe ten extents, for example. If extents can
be arbitrary in size, we are able to represent very large Ô¨Åles this way, but if extents are
typically one block long, we can deal only with relatively short Ô¨Åles.
Figure 6.11 shows the format of such a Ô¨Åle descriptor. The descriptor has room for
a ten-block Ô¨Åle. The particular Ô¨Åle it represents uses only three blocks. For simplicity,
the Ô¨Ågure omits all the other information that might be stored in the descriptor.
If a Ô¨Åle grows too large for the Ô¨Åle descriptor, we could reorganize it, at some cost,
to collapse some of its extents. That is, we could try to Ô¨Ånd free space on the disk large
enough to hold two or more extents of the Ô¨Åle. We would copy those extents into the free
space and modify the descriptor appropriately.

other
Figure 6.11 A three-block Ô¨Åle

If extents are required to be the same length (for example, one block), restructuring
will not work. Instead, we could provide overÔ¨Çow Ô¨Åle descriptors that contain more
pointers. The Ô¨Åle descriptor could use the Ô¨Årst nine pointers for the Ô¨Åle itself and, if
necessary, use the tenth pointer to point not to the next extent of the Ô¨Åle but to the Ô¨Årst
overÔ¨Çow descriptor. This overÔ¨Çow descriptor could be a full block. Assuming that we
need two 2-byte integers for each extent (one for the size, one for the pointer), we could
Ô¨Åt another 128 extents in each overÔ¨Çow descriptor. The last one could again be reserved
for overÔ¨Çow.
Figure 6.12 shows a descriptor for a Ô¨Åle that requires 13 blocks. The overÔ¨Çow

220
File Structures
Chapter 6

The structure just described works very well for sequential access, in which the
entire Ô¨Åle is read from start to Ô¨Ånish. When the Ô¨Åle is opened, the Ô¨Åle manager can read
the Ô¨Åle descriptor into main store and use its information to direct the input of the Ô¨Årst
nine extents. Then the manager can read the overÔ¨Çow Ô¨Åle descriptor into main store and
continue the input. In fact, most very long Ô¨Åles are read in exactly this fashion. They are
typically load images for large programs or data Ô¨Åles created sequentially by one pro-
gram to be read sequentially by another.
However, long Ô¨Åles used as databases are often accessed in non-sequential fashion.
As we saw, the Position service call might indicate the exact byte of the Ô¨Åle that is to be
accessed next. If this call is used, our use of extents and overÔ¨Çow Ô¨Åle descriptors has two
shortcomings.
√ò
To Ô¨Ånd out which block holds any given byte in the Ô¨Åle, we must Ô¨Årst calculate
which extent holds that byte. This calculation requires repeated inspection of the
extent entries in the descriptor because each extent may have a different size.
√ô
If many overÔ¨Çow descriptors are used, seeking a great distance in the Ô¨Åle may
require many disk operations to read all the overÔ¨Çow Ô¨Åle descriptors between the
current location and the desired location.
These problems can be addressed by a slight change in organization of the Ô¨Åle descriptor.
First, we can demand that all extents have the same length. Next, we can organize the
Ô¨Åle descriptor as a tree, not as a linked list. The tree has a depth determined by the size
of the Ô¨Åle; the depth is indicated in one of the Ô¨Åxed locations in the Ô¨Åle descriptor. A
depth-1 tree just uses the entries in the descriptor as direct pointers to extents. Figure
6.11 is a depth-1 tree. It has ten entries; therefore, depth-1 trees may use up to ten
extents.
A depth-2 tree treats each entry in the descriptor as a pointer to an overÔ¨Çow
descriptor, which is full of direct pointers to extents. If overÔ¨Çow descriptors can hold
128 pointers, such Ô¨Åles may use up to 1280 extents. A depth-3 tree treats those overÔ¨Çow
descriptors as indirect pointers yet again. Such Ô¨Åles have 10 √ó 128 √ó 128 = 163,840
extents.
Let‚Äôs assume that we have a depth-3 tree representing an enormous Ô¨Åle and we
wish to Ô¨Ånd the block that has byte 1,000,000. Since there are 512 bytes in a block, we
want byte 64 of block 1953. (The Ô¨Årst block is given the number 0.) Since each entry in
the Ô¨Åle descriptor is the root of a subtree of blocks that includes 128 √ó 128 = 16,384
blocks, the block we want is in the Ô¨Årst subtree. We now read the overÔ¨Çow descriptor
pointed to by the Ô¨Årst entry in the Ô¨Åle descriptor. Since each of its 128 entries governs
128 blocks, we want the 33rd block in the 16th entry. We read the overÔ¨Çow block
pointed to by the 16th entry. Its 33d block is the one we want. We have accessed a par-
ticular byte with four disk accesses. Some of these accesses may have been avoided by
cache hits because the disk driver caches recently used disk blocks in main store.
Our new organization does have its costs. If we do not know how long a Ô¨Åle will
be when it is created, we will have to assume it needs only a one-level tree. When it out-
grows those limits, the organization must be changed, rearranging entries (although not
recopying any data blocks). One way to avoid this rearrangement is to let the Ô¨Årst seven
entries always be direct pointers. The eighth could head a two-level tree, the ninth a
three-level tree, and the tenth a four-level tree. This scheme is pictured in Figure 6.13,
which only begins to suggest how huge the Ô¨Åle can grow.

Figure 6.13 A huge Ô¨Åle
6.3 Disk layout
The disk is divided into different regions that serve different purposes. As we have men-
tioned, subdisks can be built to promote clustering. Subdisks also allow administrators to
segregate Ô¨Åles pertaining to different projects and to limit the amount of disk space each
project uses. File descriptors occupy Ô¨Åxed locations on each subdisk. The root of a mul-
tilevel directory structure should be easy to Ô¨Ånd. One easy way to do that is to let the Ô¨Årst
descriptor in Ô¨Åle descriptor space represent the root directory. In a Ô¨Çat or two-level struc-
ture, the main directory must be similarly easy to Ô¨Ånd.
When the operating system is started, it must be able to tell the structure of the disk
by examining the disk itself. A disk descriptor could be stored at a Ô¨Åxed location (for
example, block 0) that indicates how many subdisks there are and how many blocks of
Ô¨Åle descriptor are stored on each subdisk. It would also include the bit map of free
tracks, free cylinders, or free blocks. As we have seen, free blocks might be arranged in
a linked list or a tree; pointers to this structure belong in the disk descriptor. The disk
descriptor might be stored in several places on the disk, because if the disk develops a
Ô¨Çaw, some blocks may become unreadable. It is a disaster if the only copy of the disk
descriptor becomes unreadable.
When a disk is formatted, bad sectors are detected by the disk controller and may
be sequestered so that they are no longer addressible. Occasionally, sectors become bad
after formatting. These sectors may be placed in a ‚Äò‚Äòbad-sector Ô¨Åle,‚Äô‚Äô which is otherwise
never accessed and is not placed in any directory. In this way, bad sectors are placed out
of harm‚Äôs way. Fancy archiving programs that read sectors directly from the disk
without going through directories must be aware of the bad-sector Ô¨Åle to avoid reading it.
222
File Structures
Chapter 6

6.4 Multiple disks
Even personal computers often have several disks. Each disk can have its own indepen-
dent set of Ô¨Åles, in which case the absolute Ô¨Åle name can include a disk speciÔ¨Åcation. For
example, in MS-DOS an absolute Ô¨Åle name starts with the name of the disk. For exam-
ple, one could name a Ô¨Åle A:patish.mod to indicate that it is on disk A. If the
current working directory is A:, then there is no need to use the absolute name.
On large installations, where there may be many disks, it is an inconvenience to
remember where each Ô¨Åle resides. Instead, all the disks can be treated as a single set of
Ô¨Åles. For concreteness, we will assume that a hierarchical Ô¨Åle structure is in use. We can
imagine various ways to distribute the hierarchy across disks. These methods range from
very Ô¨Åne grain to very course.
The Ô¨Ånest grain method allows individual Ô¨Åles to cross disk boundaries. Each
pointer to a data block includes both the disk identiÔ¨Åer and the block number. The list of
free blocks also spans disks. This idea is depicted in Figure 6.14. This method has
decided advantages: Mammoth Ô¨Åles such as Ô¨Åle 3 can be stored, even though no one disk
can hold them, and sequential access can be extremely fast, since seeks on some disks
can overlap transfers on others. However, all pointers to disk blocks are longer, and a
single failed disk can make almost all Ô¨Åles unreadable. Furthermore, disks such as
Ô¨Çoppies cannot be dismounted, since they no longer hold an independent set of Ô¨Åles. If
we were to remove disk C, not only would Ô¨Åle 1 disappear, but also part of Ô¨Åle 3.
At the other extreme, we can require that each disk be a self-contained subtree of
the Ô¨Åle hierarchy. Such course-grain division is used by Unix. It is shown in Figure

Mounting disks introduces novel security problems. Until a disk is mounted, it is
just a device, which in Unix can be accessed as a single device Ô¨Åle. While it is in that
state, anyone who can access that special Ô¨Åle can modify its data, introducing new access
rights and changing ownerships of the Ô¨Åles it contains. In Unix, the problem is particu-
larly severe because a malicious user can set the rights on a load image for the command
interpreter so that it is owned by the ‚Äò‚Äòsuperuser,‚Äô‚Äô an extremely privileged subject. It is
also possible to set the permissions on that Ô¨Åle so that when it runs, it acquires the rights
of its owner. Then the user can mount the disk, execute that command interpreter, and be
as powerful as the superuser. For this reason, Unix usually allows only the superuser to
call Mount or modify unmounted disks.
6.5 Multiple machines
If several machines are connected by a network, Ô¨Åles can often be accessed across
machines. A network-wide Ô¨Åle name includes its machine name. For example, in DEC-
net, a Ô¨Åle /a/b on machine George would be named George::/a/b. When a pro-
cess accesses a Ô¨Åle with a remote name, messages must be sent to a server on the remote
machine to open the Ô¨Åle there and to access it on behalf of the local process. Discovering
that a Ô¨Åle name is remote and negotiating with the remote machine can be one of the
functions of the Ô¨Åle manager (as in VMS), or it could reside outside the kernel in library
routines linked into programs that wish such access.
Just as mounting disks is an elegant way to simplify organizing Ô¨Åles on several
disks, remote mounting simpliÔ¨Åes organizing Ô¨Åles on several machines. One good
example is the Network File Server (NFS), developed by Sun Microsystems for Unix.
Instead of a local device, the Mount service call can specify a remote directory, that is, a
directory in some cooperating machine‚Äôs Ô¨Åle space. Of course, the remote machine has
the option of refusing to service such a request, and this decision can be based on the par-
ticular directory that is being mounted and the identity of the requesting machine. Once
a remote directory has been mounted, users of the local machine see a virtual Ô¨Åle hierar-
chy that includes all the Ô¨Åles in that remote directory. Whenever the local Ô¨Åle manager
needs a block from a remote machine, it sends a request across a communication line to
the remote machine.
Figure 6.17 shows a typical arrangement of Ô¨Åles across three cooperating
machines. Each machine has a disk that is used for the initial Ô¨Åle system (here, disks A,
C, and E). These disks have very similar or even identical Ô¨Åles. Each machine also has a
larger disk that holds Ô¨Åles unique to that machine (disks B, D, F). Each machine mounts
disk B onto Ô¨Åle 2, disk D onto Ô¨Åle 3, and disk F onto Ô¨Åle 4. The result is that everyone on
these three machines sees an identical extended Ô¨Åle hierarchy. In fact, if accounts are the
same on all three machines, users can log onto any machine that happens to be most

Mounting disks remotely exacerbates the security problem that we touched on ear-
lier. NFS deÔ¨Ånes the access rights of a subject making a remote access to be identical to
the rights for local access. That is, a single subject can own Ô¨Åles on various machines,
and those Ô¨Åles may be accessed as if they were local. Therefore, even if accounts are not
identical on all machines, user identiÔ¨Åers must be unique across machines. It would be
disastrous for two individuals, having accounts on machine 1 and machine 2 respectively,
to have the same user identiÔ¨Åer: They would each have rights over the other‚Äôs Ô¨Åles. Any-
one who has superuser privilege on one machine automatically has it on all machines.
For this reason, NFS makes an exception to its rule and treats the superuser as an ordi-
nary subject with respect to remote Ô¨Åles.
